    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13608482909895044753">From the quintessential author of wartime Germany, A Time to Love and a Time to Die echoes the harrowing insights of his masterpiece All Quiet on the Western Front.<br/> <br/>After two years at the Russian front, Ernst Graeber finally receives three weeks’ leave. But since leaves have been canceled before, he decides not to write his parents, fearing he would just raise their hopes.</span>
  <span id="freeText13608482909895044753" style="display:none">From the quintessential author of wartime Germany, A Time to Love and a Time to Die echoes the harrowing insights of his masterpiece All Quiet on the Western Front.<br /> <br />After two years at the Russian front, Ernst Graeber finally receives three weeks’ leave. But since leaves have been canceled before, he decides not to write his parents, fearing he would just raise their hopes.<br /> <br />Then, when Graeber arrives home, he finds his house bombed to ruin and his parents nowhere in sight. Nobody knows if they are dead or alive. As his leave draws to a close, Graeber reaches out to Elisabeth, a childhood friend. Like him, she is imprisoned in a world she did not create. But in a time of war, love seems a world away. And sometimes, temporary comfort can lead to something unexpected and redeeming.<br /> <br />“The world has a great writer in Erich Maria Remarque. He is a craftsman of unquestionably first rank, a man who can bend language to his will. Whether he writes of men or of inanimate nature, his touch is sensitive, firm, and sure.”—The New York Times Book Review</span>
  <a data-text-id="13608482909895044753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2302257413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2893812763652944062">Teleportive WWII novel, top-notch dramatization of the complexity of humanity, formal and thematic excellence throughout. It's about a German soldier who leaves the Russian front during WWII as the tide is turning for the Nazis and goes on furlough to his home city for a few weeks. He can't find his parents in the bombed-out ruins and runs into an old classmate, a comely young woman, and falls in love as, every few days, air raid sirens sound and buildings rise into the air as all hell breaks lo</span>
  <span id="freeText2893812763652944062" style="display:none">Teleportive WWII novel, top-notch dramatization of the complexity of humanity, formal and thematic excellence throughout. It's about a German soldier who leaves the Russian front during WWII as the tide is turning for the Nazis and goes on furlough to his home city for a few weeks. He can't find his parents in the bombed-out ruins and runs into an old classmate, a comely young woman, and falls in love as, every few days, air raid sirens sound and buildings rise into the air as all hell breaks loose. A completely absorbing reading experience, couldn't put it down, woke up early to read with coffee, that sort of book. Few reviews on here in English -- many in Arabic, Russian, and maybe Romanian. My third novel by this author and I have another coming tomorrow.<br /><br />Super-conventional form perfectly done, always patiently pushing ahead, therefore it feels like an organic/natural/real (ie, not imposed by the author) plot, the procession of days as a solider's on leave for a few weeks "between death and death," the first bloom of love as everything around the lovers not so much withers as it explodes and incinerates; everything super-charged by the potential arrival of devastation from above, the inevitability of horror ("a howl arose, increasing until it became maddening and unbearable, as though a huge steel planet were plunging straight at the cellar") and gruesome scenes of, for example, a five-year-old girl impaled on a shattered staircase. Streaks of gnarly description, always utilitarian and accessible prose, never clipped or degraded or showy -- the tone is perfectly centrist, flowing, poetic at times, but best of all it disappears and yields to visions of this shattered German city, its inhabitants trying to survive, everyone living not so much under the thumb of the Nazis but more so under the rule of Luck. As with his famous WWI novel and every other WWII- and Holocaust-related novel or memoir I've read, survival always depends on luck. <br /><br />But this earns the full five stars more so because it so naturally detonates Literature's primary payload: it dramatizes the complexity of humanity more clearly than most novels I've read. Not all Germans are anti-Semitic monsters intent on taking over the world and eradicating their racial inferiors. The novel depicts arch-evil types, superhuman thoughtless automaton murderers in the S.S., as well as devastated, philosophical citizens who hide Jews — and other well-characterized characters most concerned with self-preservation during the worst of times. Toward the end, it's impossible not to root for the hero Ernst even though he's fighting with the Nazis -- he's an absolutely 3D sympathetic free-thinking human being in an extraordinarily difficult situation trying to stay focused and survive even as the guts of a new recruit splatter all over him after catching a flung grenade in the stomach. <br /><br />Everyone's read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1537535139?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">All Quiet on the Western Front</a> but it seems like few have read the author's other novels, most of which were semi-recently re-published in attractive modern paperback form. The title of this one probably in part accounts for it being previously totally unknown to me -- it seems like an Ian Fleming/James Bond ripoff by way of The Byrds' appropriation of biblical verses. Alternate titles could have been "Switzerland" (not reduced to rubble and therefore often mentioned as an ideal place to escape to, although it seems impossible to get to), "An Eden in Hell" (good assonance, bad pun -- suggests a few of the spots where Ernst and Elisabeth take mental, spiritual, physical refuge and just live a normal life for a few moments), "Shelter from the Storm" (novel was published in 1957, pre-dates Dylan's song by almost two decades) -- the actual title seems a little too sentimental and monumental, a little too B-movie?<br /><br />Here's a fantastic passage where our hero Ernst and his future wife Elisabeth are sitting on a hill in a wooded area where the trees are covered in strips of tin foil that fall before air raids to jam and distort radio transmissions: "The trees around the clearing were covered with strips that fluttered from their twigs, twisting and sparkling in the breeze. The sun broke through the mountainous clouds and transformed the woods into a glittering fairyland. What once had fluttered down in the midst of ravening death and the shrill noise of destruction now hung silent and shiny on the trees and had become silver and a shimmering and the memory of childhood stories and the great festival of peace." <br /><br />"Oh man" I said as I finished it, but I don't want to spoil the end for anyone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2893812763652944062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8678142108544401212" style="display:none">I loved his “All quiet on western front”. But it was written by a younger Erich, pacifist and sad but also boyish. Do you suppose that I don’t think about it every day?”<br />***<br />Pohlmann smiled. “You are right. But there has never been a tyranny that lasted. Humanity has not advanced in an even course.           <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25109368-walter?shelf=fiction-historical-wwii">fiction-historical-wwii</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer998425938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer978346684836555922">I have always been a fan of Erich Maria Remarque's novel "All Quiet on the Western Front", which I consider to be one of the best war novels ever written. So, when I saw "A Time to Love and a Time to Die" at a used book store, I was excited to read it. I had hoped that "A Time" would live up to the high standards of "All Quiet". I was not disappointed.<br/><br/>"A Time" is arguably an even better novel than "All Quiet". Set in World War II, it is the story of Ernst Grueber, a soldier in the German Army on</span>
  <span id="freeText978346684836555922" style="display:none">I have always been a fan of Erich Maria Remarque's novel "All Quiet on the Western Front", which I consider to be one of the best war novels ever written. So, when I saw "A Time to Love and a Time to Die" at a used book store, I was excited to read it. I had hoped that "A Time" would live up to the high standards of "All Quiet". I was not disappointed.<br /><br />"A Time" is arguably an even better novel than "All Quiet". Set in World War II, it is the story of Ernst Grueber, a soldier in the German Army on the Russian Front who, after two years of constant combat, is suddenly granted a three week leave. He goes home, excited to put the devastation and privation of war behind him and enjoy the luxuries of home. But when he gets to his hometown, he finds the same blown up buildings, the same human misery, the same piles of bodies that he saw in Russia and Africa and France and other places where he had served with the Wehrmacht. The Nazis had brought the horrors of war to so many other countries since 1939. Now, the allies had taken to bombing the town, and the bombing raids brought the horror of war home to Germany. But even worse than the bombing raids, the Nazi party had turned neighbor against neighbor and brother against brother, until nobody could speak openly about anything. It seems that the Nazis destroyed Germany once, and the allies destroyed the country a second time.<br /><br />Ernst returns home to find his family home destroyed and his family missing. While he's in town he runs into Elizabeth, a young woman whom he had known in school but had never given a thought to before. Elizabeth lost her father because a Nazi had denounced him, and her father was sent to the concentration camp. So here are two disillusioned young people who have lost their families and wonder what it is all about. Why should the German people continue to suffer for the Nazis. Why can't the war end?<br /><br />Remarque is an amazing author who brings the awful reality of war home to the reader. His prose is very vivid, and his characters are likeable. He captures the silly banter of soldiers while also describing the horrors that soldiers have to live with every day. He reminds us that wars are not about ideologies or patriotism or even about national interests. They are about soldiers, human beings who long to end the suffering and return to civilian life. German soldiers hated the Nazis as much as the Allied soldiers did, perhaps even more so because they saw the Nazis who were naked opportunists who denounced their fellow citizens in order to improve their standing with the party. But like all men of honor, they fought for their country and were willing to lay down their lives for the fatherland.<br /><br />This is an amazing novel, and I highly recommend it to everyone, especially to anyone who is interested in the Second World War.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="978346684836555922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_596358135" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/596358135" />
    <a title="Veleka Georgieva" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14796545-veleka-georgieva"><img alt="Veleka Georgieva" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353794881p2/14796545.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/596358135?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veleka Georgieva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veleka Georgieva" href="/user/show/14796545-veleka-georgieva">Veleka Georgieva</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14796545-veleka-georgieva?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer596358135&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer596358135" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14257515872106429392">A Time to Love and a Time to Die is a marvelous book which, despite being very dark and hard to read at times, turned out to be my sweet escape from the real world. It issues World War II, but it can really refer to any everyday routine which seems to suck the life out of us. Because despite there being a very clear story and storyline, this book is above everything else a search for peace, for love, for hope. What I was left with in the end was not the image of the cold muddy Russian fields – I</span>
  <span id="freeText14257515872106429392" style="display:none">A Time to Love and a Time to Die is a marvelous book which, despite being very dark and hard to read at times, turned out to be my sweet escape from the real world. It issues World War II, but it can really refer to any everyday routine which seems to suck the life out of us. Because despite there being a very clear story and storyline, this book is above everything else a search for peace, for love, for hope. What I was left with in the end was not the image of the cold muddy Russian fields – I was left with that beautiful small house which resembled paradise in the middle of hell. I was left with a beautiful love story which started so suddenly and lasted so short, but which never really ended. I was left with so many questions when I turned the last page. I was sad, I was surprised, I was angry, and I was crushed having finally understood the damn title. Why couldn’t it have been A Time to Love and a Time to Fight? Why did he have to die? And why did that happen just when he finally stood up for himself, for what he believed was right and for those ungrateful wolves undercover who he thought to be innocent? <br />This book is the very example of how cruel life sometimes can be, but also of how genius an average man can be. I marvel at the change in the main character from the beginning until the end of the book. And the most horrible thing after all was the fact that only he and his wife were changed, and everything else remained the same. It didn’t matter what he believed in; it didn’t matter what he knew or didn’t know; it didn’t matter if there was someone he loved or not – there was war and he was a soldier, and that was all that mattered. The devastating truth is that Graeber was no one in the face of war, and he was no one in the face of history. And perhaps, there was more than one Graeber in the battlefield. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14257515872106429392" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4400435000360103011">I had no idea. This book belonged to my father, I believe. And has sat on my book shelf for years. I remember reading All Quiet on the Western Front ages and ages ago. In high school? I remember it being heavy, about war, and I remember beautiful writing. Maybe I needed to grow up to understand that a book about tragedy and love and being hopeful, about the ache of the precarious world, is actually the stuff of great literature, since it's what life is about. I am savoring this book. I think my </span>
  <span id="freeText4400435000360103011" style="display:none">I had no idea. This book belonged to my father, I believe. And has sat on my book shelf for years. I remember reading All Quiet on the Western Front ages and ages ago. In high school? I remember it being heavy, about war, and I remember beautiful writing. Maybe I needed to grow up to understand that a book about tragedy and love and being hopeful, about the ache of the precarious world, is actually the stuff of great literature, since it's what life is about. I am savoring this book. I think my book club will love it. <br /><br />This time of quarantine, of living without libraries (once I read the 17 books I checked out) has been a gift. I am reading through my own books and discovering so many treasures. Some, I'm rereading, but this book was a first-time read. I can't recommend it highly enough. Don't let the dreariness of the first chapters, which take place in Russia as German soldiers try to hold off the Russian advance, discourage you. The time is terrifying, but brings with it the possibility for love and connection...I love this book. I'll read it again.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33539108-ellie-midwood?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2548239241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16617713211896226680">Erich Maria Remarque is a literary genius and this WW2 novel is another proof of it. I fell in love with Remarque’s writing after reading his eternal classic, “All Quiet on the Western Front.” “A Time to Love and a Time to Die” is just as tragically beautiful and poetic, with the only difference: the protagonist, Ernst Graeber, fights in the Second World War - for the country, which hasn’t learned from its mistakes. After a few grueling years on the front, having gone through both Europe and Afr</span>
  <span id="freeText16617713211896226680" style="display:none">Erich Maria Remarque is a literary genius and this WW2 novel is another proof of it. I fell in love with Remarque’s writing after reading his eternal classic, “All Quiet on the Western Front.” “A Time to Love and a Time to Die” is just as tragically beautiful and poetic, with the only difference: the protagonist, Ernst Graeber, fights in the Second World War - for the country, which hasn’t learned from its mistakes. After a few grueling years on the front, having gone through both Europe and Africa, Ernst is longing for some time alone to make sense of this all. His furlough presents him with such an opportunity; only home is not home anymore, and the whole country has changed while he was away. Only Elisabeth, his childhood acquaintance, is still there and she appears to be one of the very few people who kept her sanity in a world that has gone mad. <br />The plot and characterization are masterful, as always. A kaleidoscope of characters, each presenting a little piece of Nazi Germany’s society, is incredibly diverse and frighteningly real. The sense of hope lost, of mistrust, of devastation, is palpable and even more suffocating opposed to our two heroes, who still have something stronger than war and death - their love for each other. Rebellious spirits who refuse to succumb to the regime and its puppets, they’re a couple you feel for and admire at the same time. A truly unforgettable novel that all historical fiction buffs will enjoy. Highly recommended! </span>
  <a data-text-id="16617713211896226680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2264272775" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2264272775" />
    <a title="Lidia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15640337-lidia"><img alt="Lidia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496843147p2/15640337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2264272775?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lidia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lidia" href="/user/show/15640337-lidia">Lidia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2264272775" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9074753144398279788">This is my second favorite Remarque book, after Arch of Triumph. Remarque's ability to portray war is uncomparable, the way he combines the beautiful with the primitive, loss and desperation with hopeless happiness and hatred with comradeship is heart wrentching. The beginning of the plot was a bit too harsh for my taste, but midway through the book I was hooked, lost alongside naïve, yet unexpectedly wise Graeber. Even though the end took me by surprise, I cannot deny liking the irony there. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3249100059" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3249100059" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22805551-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520073601p2/22805551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3249100059?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/22805551-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789542614890" href="/book/show/28001570">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3249100059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7425196713778079979">The book starts out very strong, on Russian land, cold, full of emotion and death. The love story in the middle somehow lacked depth to me. I couldn't get emotionally involved with the characters, maybe the translation was off. Throughout the whole book I sensed the typical Remarque style of writing, like the characters' souls are wasted and hopeless in a messy world, and they drink a lot of alchohol. Back in the battlefront, the ending was really enjoyable.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Igor Nadezhkin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67972428-igor-nadezhkin"><img alt="Igor Nadezhkin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496418763p2/67972428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2006967052?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Igor Nadezhkin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Igor Nadezhkin" href="/user/show/67972428-igor-nadezhkin">Igor Nadezhkin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2006967052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16631758782196548611">When I was a little boy, and my grandfather, a veteran of the Second World War, was still alive, he often said that only a fool who had never been in battle will admire the war. Real soldiers, heroes, hate war. When I read the books of Erich Maria Remarque, I seem to understand what he meant. Remarque was a real pacifist. A pacifist who held arms and killed. And that's why he could fully appreciate the importance of his life, and the life of another person. His books, this is a testimony How the</span>
  <span id="freeText16631758782196548611" style="display:none">When I was a little boy, and my grandfather, a veteran of the Second World War, was still alive, he often said that only a fool who had never been in battle will admire the war. Real soldiers, heroes, hate war. When I read the books of Erich Maria Remarque, I seem to understand what he meant. Remarque was a real pacifist. A pacifist who held arms and killed. And that's why he could fully appreciate the importance of his life, and the life of another person. His books, this is a testimony How the war has crippled the souls of a whole generation, Leaving a trace in their hearts. The characters of his books seem so alive that, seeing their death, the reader feels the pain and fear that they possess in the last moments of their lives, and for a long time can not get rid of the sense of loss. A special tragedy is the scenes of a peaceful life, far away and no longer accessible to soldiers. A life in which they could become doctors, engineers, gardeners, buy a house in a quiet neighborhood, move to a farm, marry, have dinner with their family every Sunday, see how their children and grandchildren grow. They could be anyone and do anything, but instead, their dead bodies rotted away from home, on the battlefield. And it does not matter what Remarque wrote about, about war or about love, in his characters we always see anguish and pain, a person who has seen so much evil that he will never be able to return to the ordinary life, about which he so dreamed. People go to war, but only soldiers return. And Erich Maria Remarque was a soldier until the end of his days.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16631758782196548611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/382705673?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yesenia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yesenia" href="/user/show/11508002-yesenia">Yesenia</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6913781538701694668">Brilliant on so many levels. We were studying the Stanford Prison Experiment in psychology, which led me to read Ordinary Men, which led me to read this. And I honestly think this is the better of the three for explaining why good people do bad things.<br/><br/>A soldier tries on the role of a civilian, tries to balance following orders with making his own life, and struggles with disillusionment and love. He's still a Nazi, still a German soldier, but he doesn't feel the weight of those title yet. He's j</span>
  <span id="freeText6913781538701694668" style="display:none">Brilliant on so many levels. We were studying the Stanford Prison Experiment in psychology, which led me to read Ordinary Men, which led me to read this. And I honestly think this is the better of the three for explaining why good people do bad things.<br /><br />A soldier tries on the role of a civilian, tries to balance following orders with making his own life, and struggles with disillusionment and love. He's still a Nazi, still a German soldier, but he doesn't feel the weight of those title yet. He's just carrying out orders, defending the Fatherland, doing his duty to his country. And though he rationalizes everything in the name of the greater good, being there and bearing witness prickles his conscience.<br /><br />It's very much a book about the human condition; about being human and having fears and wants and weak points.</span>

<div id="review_1844173776" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1844173776" />
    <a title="Leslie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/775264-leslie"><img alt="Leslie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1318378054p2/775264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1844173776?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leslie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leslie" href="/user/show/775264-leslie">Leslie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/775264-leslie?shelf=dad-s-books">dad-s-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1844173776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1634956876344874744">Sad to say, I don't think I've read "All Quiet on the Western Front" yet. I will have to. <br/><br/>I believe this was the first novel I have read about the German side of WWII. It starts on the Russian front as the Germans are in retreat, returns to Germany with the protagonist Graeber on furlough, and then returns to Russia to continue the war.<br/><br/>Among its striking features is that it does not glamorize war at all. In fact, the nurse in Slaughterhouse Five might have approved it as much or more than Vonneg</span>
  <span id="freeText1634956876344874744" style="display:none">Sad to say, I don't think I've read "All Quiet on the Western Front" yet. I will have to. <br /><br />I believe this was the first novel I have read about the German side of WWII. It starts on the Russian front as the Germans are in retreat, returns to Germany with the protagonist Graeber on furlough, and then returns to Russia to continue the war.<br /><br />Among its striking features is that it does not glamorize war at all. In fact, the nurse in Slaughterhouse Five might have approved it as much or more than Vonnegut's anti-war novel, although they are totally different in all other ways. The way it manages not to glamorize war is by vividly describing what the soldiers went through. As the Germans were falling back, they were digging up the frozen bodies they had left behind when they were advancing the previous fall in order to bury them; they executed helpless Russian peasants; they lived in fear of acknowledging that they knew they'd lost, as the SS would kill them for such statements (or less); as bombs fell, they cowered in cellars and the ones who weren't crushed systematically searched for and tried to dig the others out before they died; they were living on tenuous supplies of food and ammunition dropped by planes that came sporadically; they were living among blasted body parts and mud and stench and hopelessness.<br /><br />For all the horror and the violence, the book was strangely mystical and profound and jewel-like. Graeber was searching for justice, and goodness, and love, and lasting in the midst of everything. And of course there is a war-time love story. Here are some favorite passages:<br /><br />************<br />[At the home of Alfons Binding, a childhood schoolmate of Graeber's and now an enthusiastic SA commander]:<br />A couple of sparrows flew twittering to the bird bath in the middle of the lawn. One of them waded in and began to beat his wings, and all at once they were all in the bath splashing about. Alfons was watching them intently. He seemed to have forgotten Heini already. Graeber looked at the satisfied, harmless face and, with sudden shock, he realized the eternal hopelessness to which justice and sympathy are condemned: always to suffer shipwreck on egoism and indifference and fear - he realized it and he realized, too, that he himself was not exempt, that he too was caught in ti in an anonymous, indirect, and sinister fashion, as though he and Binding somehow belonged together, however much he might struggle against it.<br /><br />*******************<br />[At Elisabeth's apartment during their courtship.]<br />The orb was rising, distended and red, over the roofs opposite.It was like a monster with a glowing head eatings its way into the street. Graeber took two water glasses and filled them halfway with cognac. He handed one to Elisabeth. "Let's drink this now," he said. "Wine is no good in the dark."<br />The moon rose higher and became more peaceful and more golden. They lay for a time in silence. Elisabeth turned her head. "What are we really," she asked, "happy or unhappy?"<br />Graeber reflected. "We are both. And probably that's how it has to be today. Unmixed happiness in our times belongs only to cows. Or not event o them any more. Perhaps only to stones."<br />Elisabeth looked at Graeber. "That doesn't matter either, does it?"<br />"No."<br />"Does anything matter?"<br />"Yes." Graeber looked into the cold, golden light that was slowly filling the room. "We are no longer dead," he said. "And we are not yet dead."<br /><br />*************************<br />[A clumsy marriage proposal, continued.]<br />Elisabeth did not move. "Why do you want to marry me?" she murmured. "After all you hardly know me."<br />"I have known you long enough."<br />"How long? A few days."<br />"Not a few days. I've known you for over a year. That's long enough."<br />"Why a year? We can't count the time from our childhood; that was too long ago."<br />"I'm not counting that either. But I was given about three weeks' furlough for two years in the field. I have been here almost two weeks ow. That corresponds to about fifteen months at the front. And so I have known you for what amounts to more than a year: the equivalent of almost two weeks' leave."<br />Elisabeth opened her eyes. "I have never thought about it that way."<br />"I haven't either. I thought of that just now."<br />"When?"<br />"Earlier while you were asleep. In the rain and the dark you think of lots of things."<br />Does it have to be rainy and dark for that?"<br />"No. But one thinks differently then."<br />"Did you think of anything else."<br />"Yes. It occurred to me how marvelous it is htat a man could use his hands and arms for something beside shooting a gun and throwing grenades."<br />She looked at him. "Why didn't you tell me that at noon today?"<br />"At noontime one can't say that."<br />"It would have been better than talking about monthly allowances and marriage subsidies."<br />Graeber lifted his head. "It was the same thing, Elisabeth," he said. "Only in different words."<br />She murmured something. "Words are sometimes very important," she said then. "At least in something like this."<br />"I'm not used to using them so. But I will find some more of them. All I need is a little time."<br />"Time," Elisabeth sighed. "We don't have much, do we?"<br />"No. Yesterday we still had a lot. And tomorrow we will think that today we had a lot."<br />Graeber lay still. Elisabeth's head rested on his arm, her hair flowed darkly over the white pillow and the shadows of the rain moved across her face. "You want to marry me," she murmured. "But do you even know whether you love me at all?"<br />"How could we know that? One needs much more time and being together for that."<br />"Perhaps. But then why do you want to marry me?"<br />"Because I can no longer think of life without you."<br />Elisabeth was silent for a time. "Don't you believe that what's happened to us could have happened to you with somebody else?" she asked then.<br />Graeber looked at the wavering gray tapestry woven by the rain outside the window. "Perhaps it could have happened to me with somebody else," he said. "Who can tell about things like that? Only now that it has happened to us I can't image the possibility that it could ever have happened to me with anyone else."<br />Elisabeth moved her head in his arm. "You're learning. You're not talking now the way you did at noon. But of course it's night. Do you think I'll have to wait all my life for it to be night?"<br /><br />*****************<br />[During a visit to an old schoolteacher who is under suspicion of the Gestapo]<br />"I've thought a great deal about you. And I've thought about what you said to me recently. There's no answer." Pohlmann hesitated and then said softly: "Only one. You must believe. What else remains?"<br />"Believe in what?"<br />"In God. And in what's good in men."<br />"Haven't you ever doubted that?" Graeber asked.<br />"Of course," the old man replied. "Often. How else could I believe?"<br /><br />*****************<br />[Revisiting Pohlmann]<br />The old man was silent for a time. Then he walked over to the globe that stood in the corner and spun it. "Look here," he said. "This tiny piece of the world is Germany. You can almost cover it with your thumb. It's a very small part of the world."<br />"That may be. But from this small part we have conquered a large slice of the world."<br />"A slice, yes. And conquered - but not convinced."<br />"Not yet. But what would have happened if we had been able to hold onto it? Ten years. Or twenty. Or fifty. Victories and successes are horribly effective persuaders. We have seen that in our own country."<br />"We were not victorious."<br />"That's no proof."<br />"It is a proof," Pohlmann said. "A very profound one." His hand with the thick veins continued to turn the globe. "The world," he said. "The world does not stand still. When one despairs for a time of his own country he must believe in the world. An eclipse is possible but not an enduring period of night. Not on this planet. One must not make things easier for oneself by simply giving way to despair."<br />He pushed the globe back. "You ask whether there's enough left to begin over again. The Church began with a few fishermen, a few of the faithful in the catacombs, and the survivors of the arenas of Rome."<br />"Yes. And the Nazis with a few jobless fanatics in a beer hall in Munich."<br />Pohlmann smiled. "You are right. But there has never been a tyranny that lasted. Humanity has not advanced in an even course. It has always been only by thrusts, jerks, relapses, and spams. We were too arrogant; we thought we had already conquered our blood past. Now we know that we dare not so much as look around for fear it will get hold of us again." He picked up his hat. "I must go."<br />"Here's your book about Switzerland," Graeber said. "It's rather the worse for rain. I lost it, but I found it again and rescued it."<br />"You need not have rescued it. One doesn't have to rescue dreams."<br />"On the contrary," Graeber said. "What else?"<br />"Faith. Dreams always reshape themselves."<br />"One hopes so. Otherwise you might as well hang yourself."<br />"How young you still are!" Pohlmann said. "But what am I saying? You really are still very young." He put on his overcoat. "Strange - I had always pictured youth differently -"<br />"So had I," Graeber said.<br /><br />******************<br />[After a visit to the Gestapo headquarters]<br />He felt the cigar box under his arm. Very likely it did not contain Kruse's ashes at all. Holtmann could easily have made a mistake; and it was hardly to be assumed that people in a concentration camp took much pains with such matters. Besides, it would not be possible with mass incinerations. Some stoker would shovel out a few handfuls of ashes and pack them up, that was all. Graeber could not understand why it was done at all. It was a mixture of inhumanity and bureaucracy which made the inhumanity all the more inhumane.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1634956876344874744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3376602905" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3376602905" />
    <a title="David Hines" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3066262-david-hines"><img alt="David Hines" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392509482p2/3066262.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3376602905?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Hines" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Hines" href="/user/show/3066262-david-hines">David Hines</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780449912508" href="/book/show/164062.A_Time_to_Love_and_a_Time_to_Die">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3376602905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15750435682707081141">Remarque is best known for "All Quiet on the Western Front" his masterpiece novel of World War I, but he also wrote a number of additional novels, almost all of which are very good, and it's a shame they are not as well known today as his masterpiece. While most of his work is set in the time of World War I and shortly after, this book is set in the final days of World War II. A German soldier, once very patriotic and into his nation's Hitler-led fight, has come to realize that the war is lost a</span>
  <span id="freeText15750435682707081141" style="display:none">Remarque is best known for "All Quiet on the Western Front" his masterpiece novel of World War I, but he also wrote a number of additional novels, almost all of which are very good, and it's a shame they are not as well known today as his masterpiece. While most of his work is set in the time of World War I and shortly after, this book is set in the final days of World War II. A German soldier, once very patriotic and into his nation's Hitler-led fight, has come to realize that the war is lost and Russia is slowly pushing the Germans back into defeat. He is finally allowed to go home on leave, and is shocked to find his hometown bombed by the Allies, his parents missing, and his town still being regularly attacked, information unknown to the front-line soldiers, who all think the homeland is safe even as they struggle. <br /><br />The main character finds the Gestappo everywhere, and people in fear of them. He tries to find information out about his parents, only to be stifled by the insane German bueracracy, struggling to retain their storied order even as the town is blown up around them. He comes across a young woman he once slightly knew and didn't find attractive, yet over the course of the book the two of them become a couple and even do a shotgun marriage. The protagonist encounters a childhood acquaintence he barely knew who is now a high ranking German brownshirt, and the man befriends him and helps him even though the main character can barely tolerate him. <br /><br />Somehow Remarque captures the final days of World War II as realistically as he depicted World War I, and this book, although not often seen in bookstores today, is a masterpiece. In the end, the character realizes that war is always lived through practical adaption and you also have to indiscriminately forget morals and kill or be killed. This book is not a kind book. But it is a powerful and realistic depiction of war. Highly recommended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15750435682707081141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3072702299?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dakota" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dakota" href="/user/show/10657881-dakota">Dakota</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10657881-dakota?shelf=books-to-read-before-i-die">books-to-read-before-i-die</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3072702299" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14426245823891551580">Remarque’s is best known for “All Quite on the Western Front”, but there are more equally great stories to follow! “A Time to Love and a Time to Die” is one of them. Remarque takes his reader to the Second World War this time and leads them not through a battle field but a bombed out German city. A young soldier on leave from the Russian front is left alone in the ruined streets of his home town, but beauty is found in ugliness of it all. <br/><br/>Naturally, Remarque leaves his readers frustrated in the </span>
  <span id="freeText14426245823891551580" style="display:none">Remarque’s is best known for “All Quite on the Western Front”, but there are more equally great stories to follow! “A Time to Love and a Time to Die” is one of them. Remarque takes his reader to the Second World War this time and leads them not through a battle field but a bombed out German city. A young soldier on leave from the Russian front is left alone in the ruined streets of his home town, but beauty is found in ugliness of it all. <br /><br />Naturally, Remarque leaves his readers frustrated in the end but your left wanting more of his stories. Relaxing and emotionally engaging at the same time.</span>

<div id="review_1487591173" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1487591173" />
    <a title="Cerisaye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15718667-cerisaye"><img alt="Cerisaye" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1487591173?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cerisaye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cerisaye" href="/user/show/15718667-cerisaye">Cerisaye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21284919-a-time-to-love-and-a-time-to-die">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15718667-cerisaye?shelf=historical-fiction-ww-2-germany">historical-fiction-ww-2-germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15718667-cerisaye?shelf=fiction-in-translation-german">fiction-in-translation-german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15718667-cerisaye?shelf=war-fiction">war-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1487591173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6339923954590825835">Beautifully written with a mix of plain, straight forward prose and passages of lyricism and philosophical contemplation of what war does to combatants in the field, civilians, and loved ones at home far from the front. This is a devastating story, its title tells you what to expect; therefore, there's an air of inevitability with the only question being how not if it will happen (though I wanted very much to be wrong). <br/><br/>I am not a huge fan of romance stories yet the love story part of the novel </span>
  <span id="freeText6339923954590825835" style="display:none">Beautifully written with a mix of plain, straight forward prose and passages of lyricism and philosophical contemplation of what war does to combatants in the field, civilians, and loved ones at home far from the front. This is a devastating story, its title tells you what to expect; therefore, there's an air of inevitability with the only question being how not if it will happen (though I wanted very much to be wrong). <br /><br />I am not a huge fan of romance stories yet the love story part of the novel affected me deeply. Ernst Graeber and Elisabeth have a very short time together while the young <i>Wehrmacht</i>soldier is on home leave in an unnamed German city subjected to regular, devastating air raids in the spring of 1944. They experience the joy of love &amp; life, then the pain of separation. <br /><br />Remarque effectively shows the impact of fighting upon the bodies of soldiers and the horrific results of bombing raids and incendiaries on German civilians. Some graphic passages make difficult reading, and serve as a timely reminder just what it means to drop bombs onto towns and cities full of women and children, no matter how effective this might be as a strategy in winning a war. <blockquote>
  <i>It was over. The city stank of burning and death and was full of fires. There were red and green and yellow and white ones; some were nothing but crawling, serpent-like flickerings over the fallen ruins, and others glared steadily out of roofs up toward the sky; there were fires that wrapped themselves almost tenderly around the still-standing house fronts, embracing them closely, shyly, cautiously, and others that shot violently out of windows. There were conflagrations and fiery walls and fiery towers, there were blazing dead and there were blazing wounded who burst shrieking out of the houses and spun round and tried to climb up the sides of walls and ran raving in circles until they collapsed and crept, mewling hoarsely, and then only jerked and croaked and stank of burned death.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Remarque shows us the worst humankind can do but, too, the small gestures of kindness and mercy, helpfulness and generosity. You get a good idea what it was like to live in fear of bombs and the ever-present danger from informers and the <i>Gestapo</i>. Party officials and hangers-on enjoy luxuries and comforts denied the ordinary population subject to rationing and hardship. Ernst knows the war is lost, serving on the eastern front as the German retreat turns to flight before the advancing Russian army. <blockquote> <i>All at once he had begun to think. He and many others. That was easy and cheap. As long as they had been victorious everything had seemed to be in order, and whatever was not in order was overlooked or excused because of the great goal. What goal? Had there not always been two sides to it? And had not one of them been from the start dark and inhuman? Why hadn't he thought about that sooner?</i></blockquote> <br /><br />With retreat comes the frightening realisation that what they have done to Russia and other occupied territories will now be done to Germany, there is no longer any difference between the front and home. <blockquote> <i>Just what had he expected? An island behind the fronts? Home, security, refuge, comfort? Perhaps. But the islands of refuge had long since silently sunk under the monotony of pointless death, the fronts were shattered and the war was everywhere. Everywhere, even in the brain and the heart.</i></blockquote> <br /><br />Normal pre-war life becomes like a beautiful dream, a world without destruction, death and fear. Ernst's three weeks with Elisabeth restore his abilities to think, to feel, to enjoy life again. And also to consider what he had refused to know in order to quiet his conscience, something his reawakening will no longer tolerate. Graeber is troubled by questions of guilt, complicity and responsibility. How can he go back to the front to rejoin his unit? <blockquote>
  <i>How far shall I be an accomplice if I know not only that the war is lost but also that we have to lose it so that slavery, murder, concentration camps, S.S. and S.D. mass exterminations and inhumanity shall cease...?</i>
</blockquote> A young soldier like Graeber who grew up indoctrinated by the Nazis and who would be shot, reprisals taken against his family, should he decide to desert his duty to the Fatherland. <br /><br />This is as much an anti-war novel as Remarque's more famous <i>All Quiet on the Western Front</i>. Its German perspective offers interesting reading to those of us who grew up in the UK and other Allied countries, and it is not that usual to move from horrific depiction of frontline atrocities to harrowing scenes of injury and death at home. Soldiers are told they must not give away the extent of the army's retreat and disarray, meanwhile mothers write letters to soldier sons that omit any mention of devastating bombing raids and food shortages. Remarque makes a good case for the necessity to make the most of whatever time we have, that intense living no matter how brief is worth the pain of its loss, the fear that replaces indifference when one has something, someone to live for. A book that shines a little light on the darker aspects of human nature and our ability to inflict pain and suffering on others, and, above all, the full, appalling cost of war to all those involved. Very highly recommended to any readers interested in the Second World War and the German experience from an insider's perspective.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6339923954590825835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2846182740" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2846182740" />
    <a title="Carlotta M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68362581-carlotta-m"><img alt="Carlotta M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496676184p2/68362581.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2846182740?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carlotta M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carlotta M" href="/user/show/68362581-carlotta-m">Carlotta M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783462027266" href="/book/show/1516335.Zeit_zu_leben_und_Zeit_zu_sterben">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2846182740" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14530900015025218531">Epic.Rare. And very very real.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1840030186" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1840030186" />
    <a title="Edita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5685453-edita"><img alt="Edita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595755309p2/5685453.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1840030186?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edita" href="/user/show/5685453-edita">Edita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780449912508" href="/book/show/164062.A_Time_to_Love_and_a_Time_to_Die">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1840030186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9096754940430421800">There was nothing that one could rely on; nothing that offered warmth. <br/>*<br/>The past. There's nothing we can do with it. It simply weighs us down. Even what was good in it. We must begin again. The past is bankrupt. We can't go back.<br/>*<br/>The realization that they would have to part had struck in front of him like a dark meteor. He had known it all the time, the way one knows many things—without actually realizing them or completely feeling them. There had always been so much in between. Now all at once </span>
  <span id="freeText9096754940430421800" style="display:none">There was nothing that one could rely on; nothing that offered warmth. <br />*<br />The past. There's nothing we can do with it. It simply weighs us down. Even what was good in it. We must begin again. The past is bankrupt. We can't go back.<br />*<br />The realization that they would have to part had struck in front of him like a dark meteor. He had known it all the time, the way one knows many things—without actually realizing them or completely feeling them. There had always been so much in between. Now all at once it was there by itself, big and full of a chill horror, and it was radiating a pale, penetrating, skeletonizing light —like X-rays that pierce through the charm and magic of life, leaving nothing but the bare residue and the inevitable.<br />*<br />At night one is what he was intended to be; not what he has become. <br />*<br />Nothing was there but the clear pain of loss. It was a loss forever. Nowhere was there a bridge. He had had it and it was lost. He listened inward. Somewhere there must still be a voice; an echo of hope must still linger somewhere. But he found nothing. There were only emptiness and nameless pain.<br />*<br /> Suddenly everything seemed very strange and all connections dissolved. Graeber knew the feeling; he had often had it when he woke up at night not knowing where he was. It was as if he had fallen out of the world and floated in complete loneliness in the dark. It never lasted long. One always found one's way back; but each time a strange small feeling persisted that some day one would not find the way back.<br />*<br />He felt Elisabeth's letters in his pocket. Warmth was in them, tenderness and the sweet excitement of love. But they were no quiet lamp to light a well-ordered house; they were will-o'-the-wisps above a swamp, and the farther he tried to follow them the more treacherous the swamp seemed to become. He had wanted to put up a light in order to find his way back, but he had put it up before the house was built. He had placed it in a ruin; it did not adorn it, it only made it more desolate. Back there he had not known. He had followed the light without question, wanting to believe that to follow it was enough. It was not enough. <br /> <br />He had fought against this realization as long as he could. It had not been easy to see that what he had hoped would hold him and support him had only isolated him. It could not extend far enough. It touched his heart but it did not hold him. It was swallowed up; it was a small, private happiness that could not support itself in the limitless morass of general misery and despair. He took out Elisabeth's letters and read them, and the red afterglow of sunset lay on the pages. He knew them by heart; he read them once more, and again they made him more lonesome than before. It had been too short and the other was too long. It had been a furlough; but a soldier's life is reckoned by his time at the front and not by furloughs.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9096754940430421800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2397123831" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2397123831" />
    <a title="Essie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58660319-essie"><img alt="Essie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587982738p2/58660319.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2397123831?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Essie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Essie" href="/user/show/58660319-essie">Essie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58660319-essie?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2397123831" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18445563722932789886">This book is an underrated masterpiece that is just as valuable as Remarque's more famous novel All Quiet on the Western Front. <br/><br/>Remarque is a master in depicting the horrors of war and its effects on the life of the average person. While in All Quiet on the Western Front the attention is focused mostly on soldiers at the front, here we see the desperate tries of the small person to live a peaceful life in war time. <br/><br/>Ernst Graeber's story is the story of millions of soldiers who came back home to </span>
  <span id="freeText18445563722932789886" style="display:none">This book is an underrated masterpiece that is just as valuable as Remarque's more famous novel All Quiet on the Western Front. <br /><br />Remarque is a master in depicting the horrors of war and its effects on the life of the average person. While in All Quiet on the Western Front the attention is focused mostly on soldiers at the front, here we see the desperate tries of the small person to live a peaceful life in war time. <br /><br />Ernst Graeber's story is the story of millions of soldiers who came back home to chaos and destruction. War takes lives, separates families, desroys homes and makes the survivors' lives a living hell. <br /><br />But even in these desperate times, we see a ray of hope. And that is love. Love which never ceases to exist. Love which can be found even in the most evil of times. The two main characters, Ernst and Elizabeth, find love suddenly, unexpectedly, in a place where seeking love is hopeless because all beautiful things are doomed for destruction. Nevertheless, they decide to hold on to that feeling and to live while they can. They desperately try to make their lives feel as normal as possible. Each sees in the other comfort, beauty and hope for a better future. <br /><br />The reader knows what the ending holds from the very title of the book. Sadly, beautiful things cannot exist long in this time of death and destruction. <br /><br />A Time to Love and a Time to Die touched me deeply. It is a very moving book that contains important themes that are still relevant today such as the importance of seeking and holding on to happiness, even if it is short-lived. I think this novel deserves a place next to the big classics. It is a book that everyone should definitely take the time to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18445563722932789886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iK6OO3/gfK749VvWhDKV+eEV7WMx/OFHgD2Al8RFH9B2xxrqqnuOGxI8FNlKs/31EnbeVMex+S+D/H9QKRhZ+Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Rol5jUGig/mJvbfU31xcZmwEKf1HBFwyEEtFk5cXJIm44O1clDlxTGN0+NsR3TRqn2cayrFJRFoTirpUekpioA==')})
  }
//]]>

Donatas Paulauskas šioje knygoje bando griauti apie šį judėjimą sklandančius mitus, pasitelkdamas tikras moterų istorijas ir lietuviškus pavyzdžius.\n\nKas pasikeitė moterų teisių srityje nuo Žemaitės 1907 m. siūlymo „sulyginti moterų ir vyrų teises“ iki šių dienų „Moterys kalba“ projekto „#Superherojės“? Kodėl šiandien feminizmas reikalingas ir moterims, ir vyrams? Kaip keisti save ir visuomenę, kad mūsų gyvenime būtų daugiau laisvės, pagarbos ir įvairovės?\n\n„Turbūt labiausiai noriu, kad ši knyga pasiektų tą skaitytoją, kuris jau žino (ar yra girdėjęs), kodėl moterys kilusios iš Veneros, o vyrai iš Marso, kodėl vyrai meluoja, o moterys verkia, kodėl vyrai nesiklauso, o moterys nesiorientuoja žemėlapiuose, kodėl vyrai myli kales, o moterys – liurbius, kokios yra „7 intymiausios vyrų paslaptys“, „9 didžiausios santykių klaidos“ ir „15 stulbinančių būdų jį pamaloninti“. Šiems skaitytojams mano knyga žada didžiausią staigmeną.“ - Donatas Paulauskas<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8962838868192580623\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque">About Erich Maria Remarque</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1207351165p3/4116.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4116.Erich_Maria_Remarque">Erich Maria Remarque
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,854
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer896166940318028189">Erich Maria Remarque (pen name of Erich Paul Remark) is one of the best known and most widely read authors of German literature in the twentieth century. <br/><br/>Remarque's biography is essentially marked and his writing fundamentally influenced by German history of the twentieth century: Childhood and youth in imperial Osnabrück, World War I, the Weimar Republic, and most of all his exile in Switzerland </span>
  <span id="freeText896166940318028189" style="display:none">Erich Maria Remarque (pen name of Erich Paul Remark) is one of the best known and most widely read authors of German literature in the twentieth century. <br /><br />Remarque's biography is essentially marked and his writing fundamentally influenced by German history of the twentieth century: Childhood and youth in imperial Osnabrück, World War I, the Weimar Republic, and most of all his exile in Switzerland and the United States. With the novel All Quiet On the Western Front, first published in 1929, Remarque attained world-wide recognition continuing today. <br /><br />Examples of his other novels also internationally published are: The Road Back (1931), Three Comrades (1936, 38), Arch of Triumph (1945), The Black Obelisk (1956), and Night in Lisbon (1962). <br /><br />Remarque's novels have been translated in more than fifty languages; globally the total edition comes up to several million copies. <br /><br />The complete works of Remarque are both highly interrelated with his Osnabrück background and speaking thematically of a critical examination of German history, whereby the preservation of human dignity and humanity in times of oppression, terror and war always was at the forefront of his literary creation. <br /><br />AKA:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17900575._________________" title="Έριχ Μαρία Ρεμάρκ" rel="nofollow noopener">Έριχ Μαρία Ρεμάρκ</a> (Greek)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8106923._________________" title="Эрих Мария Ремарк" rel="nofollow noopener">Эрих Мария Ремарк</a> (Russian)</span>
  <a data-text-id="896166940318028189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
